<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Biofuel</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">type of biological fuel from which energy is derived</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p>A <b>biofuel</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> that is produced through contemporary biological processes, such as agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digestion" title="Anaerobic digestion">anaerobic digestion</a>, rather than a fuel produced by geological processes such as those involved in the formation of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, such as coal and petroleum, from prehistoric biological matter.
</p><p>Biofuels can be derived directly from plants (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Energy_crop" title="Energy crop">energy crops</a>), or indirectly from agricultural, commercial, domestic, and/or industrial wastes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Renewable biofuels generally involve contemporary <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixation</a>, such as those that occur in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">plants</a> or <a href="/wiki/Microalgae" title="Microalgae">microalgae</a> through the process of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. Other renewable biofuels are made through the use or conversion of <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>  (referring to recently living organisms, most often referring to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">plants</a> or plant-derived materials). This biomass can be converted to convenient energy-containing substances in three different ways: thermal conversion, chemical conversion, and biochemical conversion. This biomass conversion can result in fuel in <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> form. This new biomass can also be used directly for biofuels.
</p><p>Biofuels are in theory <a href="/wiki/Carbon-neutral_fuel" title="Carbon-neutral fuel">carbon-neutral</a> because the carbon dioxide that is absorbed by the plants is equal to the carbon dioxide that is released when the fuel is burned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> However, in practice, whether or not a biofuel is carbon-neutral also depends greatly on whether the land which is used to grow the biofuel (with 1st and 2nd generation biofuel) <a href="/wiki/Land_use,_land-use_change,_and_forestry" title="Land use, land-use change, and forestry">needed to be cleared of carbon-holding vegetation</a> or not.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ethanol#Fuel" title="Ethanol">Bioethanol</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> made by <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermentation</a>, mostly from <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> produced in <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> crops such as <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Sweet_sorghum" title="Sweet sorghum">sweet sorghum</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">Cellulosic biomass</a>, derived from non-food sources, such as trees and grasses, is also being developed as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedstock" title="Feedstock">feedstock</a> for ethanol production. Ethanol can be used as a fuel for vehicles in its pure form (E100), but it is usually used as a <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_additive" title="Fuel additive">additive</a> to increase octane and improve vehicle emissions. Bioethanol is widely used in the <a href="/wiki/Biofuel_in_the_United_States" title="Biofuel in the United States">United States</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="Ethanol fuel in Brazil">Brazil</a>. Current plant design does not provide for converting the <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a> portion of plant raw materials to fuel components by fermentation.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a> can be used as a <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> for vehicles in its pure form (B100), but it is usually used as a <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> additive to reduce levels of particulates, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> from diesel-powered vehicles. Biodiesel is produced from oils or fats using <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a> and is the most common biofuel in Europe.
</p><p>In 2010, worldwide biofuel production reached 105 billion liters (28 billion gallons US), up 17% from 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biofuels2010_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biofuels2010-3">[3]</a></sup> and biofuels provided 2.7% of the world's fuels for road transport. Global <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol fuel</a> production reached 86 billion liters (23 billion gallons US) in 2010, with the United States and Brazil as the world's top producers, accounting together for about 90% of global production. The world's largest biodiesel producer is the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, accounting for 53% of all biodiesel production in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biofuels2010_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Biofuels2010-3">[3]</a></sup> As of  2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Biofuel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, mandates for blending biofuels exist in 31 countries at the national level and in 29 states or provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren212011_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> has a goal for biofuels to meet more than a quarter of world demand for transportation fuels by 2050 to reduce dependence on petroleum and coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The production of biofuels also led into a flourishing automotive industry, where by 2010, 79% of all cars produced in Brazil were made with a hybrid fuel system of bioethanol and gasoline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are various social, economic, environmental and technical <a href="/wiki/Issues_relating_to_biofuels" title="Issues relating to biofuels">issues relating to biofuels</a> production and use, which have been debated in the popular media and scientific journals.
</p>
