<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Generations">Generations</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First-generation_biofuels">First-generation biofuels</span></h3>
<p>"First-generation" or conventional biofuels are biofuels made from food crops grown on arable land. With this biofuel production generation, food crops are thus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_vs_fuel" title="Food vs fuel">explicitly grown for fuel production</a>, and not anything else. The sugar, starch, or vegetable oil obtained from the crops is converted into biodiesel or ethanol, using transesterification, or yeast fermentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bio_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-bio-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second-generation_biofuels">Second-generation biofuels</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second-generation_biofuels" title="Second-generation biofuels">Second-generation biofuels</a></div>
<p>Second generation biofuels are fuels manufactured from various types of <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>.  Biomass is a wide-ranging term meaning any source of organic carbon that is renewed rapidly as part of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>. Biomass is derived from plant materials, but can also include animal materials.
</p><p>Whereas first generation biofuels are made from the <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oils</a> found in arable crops, second generation biofuels are made from <a href="/wiki/Lignocellulosic_biomass" title="Lignocellulosic biomass">lignocellulosic biomass</a> or woody crops, agricultural residues or waste plant material (from food crops that have already fulfilled their food purpose).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The feedstock used to generate second-generation biofuels thus either grows on <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable lands</a>, but are just byproducts of the actual harvest (main crop) or they are grown on lands which cannot be used to effectively grow food crops<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and in some cases neither extra water or fertilizer is applied to them. Non-human food second generation feedstock sources include grasses, jatropha and other seed crops, waste vegetable oil, municipal solid waste and so forth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>This has both advantages and disadvantages. The advantage is that, unlike with regular food crops, no arable land is used solely for the production of fuel. The disadvantage is that unlike with regular food crops, it may be rather difficult to extract the fuel. For instance, a series of physical and chemical treatments might be required to convert lignocellulosic biomass to liquid fuels suitable for transportation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third-generation_biofuels">Third-generation biofuels</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Algaculture" title="Algaculture">Algaculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algae_fuel" title="Algae fuel">Algae fuel</a></div>
<p>From 1978 to 1996, the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">US NREL</a> experimented with using algae as a biofuels source in the "<a href="/wiki/Aquatic_Species_Program" title="Aquatic Species Program">Aquatic Species Program</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NREL_biofuels_algae_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-NREL_biofuels_algae-17">[17]</a></sup> A self-published article by Michael Briggs, at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_New_Hampshire" title="University of New Hampshire">UNH</a> Biofuels Group, offers estimates for the realistic replacement of all <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle" title="Motor vehicle">vehicular</a> fuel with biofuels by using algae that have a natural oil content greater than 50%, which Briggs suggests can be grown on algae ponds at <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">wastewater treatment</a> plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briggs2004_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs2004-18">[18]</a></sup> This oil-rich algae can then be extracted from the system and processed into biofuels, with the dried remainder further reprocessed to create ethanol. The production of algae to harvest oil for biofuels has not yet been undertaken on a commercial scale, but <a href="/wiki/Feasibility_study" title="Feasibility study">feasibility studies</a> have been conducted to arrive at the above yield estimate. In addition to its projected high yield, algaculture – unlike <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">crop-based</a> biofuels – does not entail a decrease in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_production" title="Food production">food production</a>, since it requires neither <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farmland_(farming)" title="Farmland (farming)">farmland</a> nor <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a>. Many companies are pursuing algae bioreactors for various purposes, including scaling up biofuels production to commercial levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Prof. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rodrigo_E._Teixeira&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rodrigo E. Teixeira (page does not exist)">Rodrigo E. Teixeira</a> from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama_in_Huntsville" title="University of Alabama in Huntsville">University of Alabama in Huntsville</a> demonstrated the extraction of biofuels lipids from wet algae using a simple and economical reaction in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionic_liquids" title="Ionic liquids">ionic liquids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourth-generation_biofuels">Fourth-generation biofuels</span></h3>
<p>Similarly to third-generation biofuels, fourth-generation biofuels are made using non-arable land. However, unlike third-generation biofuels, they do not require the destruction of biomass. This class of biofuels includes <a href="/wiki/Electrofuel" title="Electrofuel">electrofuels</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bio_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-bio-7">[7]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_fuel" title="Solar fuel">photobiological solar fuels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Some of these fuels are <a href="/wiki/Carbon-neutral_fuel#Production" title="Carbon-neutral fuel">carbon-neutral</a>. The conversion of crude oil from the plant seeds into useful fuels is called <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<p>The following fuels can be produced using first, second, third or fourth-generation biofuel production procedures. Most of these can even be produced using two or three of the different biofuel generation procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biogas">Biogas</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biogas_pipes.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Biogas_pipes.JPG/150px-Biogas_pipes.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Biogas_pipes.JPG/225px-Biogas_pipes.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Biogas_pipes.JPG/300px-Biogas_pipes.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biogas_pipes.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pipes carrying biogas</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">Biogas</a></div>
<p>Biogas is <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> produced by the process of <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digestion" title="Anaerobic digestion">anaerobic digestion</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_material" title="Organic material">organic material</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobe" title="Anaerobe">anaerobes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> It can be produced either from <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable_waste" title="Biodegradable waste">biodegradable waste</a> materials or by the use of <a href="/wiki/Energy_crop" title="Energy crop">energy crops</a> fed into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digester" title="Anaerobic digester">anaerobic digesters</a> to supplement gas yields. The solid byproduct, <a href="/wiki/Digestate" title="Digestate">digestate</a>, can be used as a biofuel or a fertilizer.
</p><p>Biogas can be recovered from <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_biological_treatment" title="Mechanical biological treatment">mechanical biological treatment</a> waste processing systems. <a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">Landfill gas</a>, a less clean form of biogas, is produced in <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">landfills</a> through naturally occurring anaerobic digestion. If it escapes into the atmosphere, it is a potential <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>.
</p><p>Farmers can produce biogas from <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a> from their cattle by using anaerobic digesters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syngas">Syngas</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gasification" title="Gasification">Gasification</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">Syngas</a>, a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and other hydrocarbons, is produced by partial combustion of biomass, that is, combustion with an amount of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> that is not sufficient to convert the biomass completely to carbon dioxide and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evans_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-evans-26">[26]</a></sup> Before partial combustion, the biomass is dried, and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysed</a>. The resulting gas mixture, syngas, is more efficient than direct combustion of the original biofuel; more of the energy contained in the fuel is extracted.
</p><p>Syngas may be burned directly in internal combustion engines, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbines" title="Turbines">turbines</a> or high-temperature fuel cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-e-collection.ethbib.ethz.ch_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-e-collection.ethbib.ethz.ch-27">[27]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wood_gas_generator" title="Wood gas generator">wood gas generator</a>, a wood-fueled gasification reactor, can be connected to an internal combustion engine.
</p><p>Syngas can be used to produce <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_ether" title="Dimethyl ether">DME</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, or converted via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fischer-Tropsch_process" title="Fischer-Tropsch process">Fischer-Tropsch process</a> to produce a diesel substitute, or a mixture of alcohols that can be blended into gasoline. Gasification normally relies on temperatures greater than 700 °C.
</p><p>Lower-temperature gasification is desirable when co-producing <a href="/wiki/Biochar" title="Biochar">biochar</a>, but results in syngas polluted with <a href="/wiki/Tar" title="Tar">tar</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethanol">Ethanol</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">Ethanol fuel</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg/220px-Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg/330px-Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg/440px-Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sao_Paulo_ethanol_pump_04_2008_74_zoom.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Common_ethanol_fuel_mixtures#E100" title="Common ethanol fuel mixtures">Neat ethanol</a> on the left (A), <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> on the right (G) at a <a href="/wiki/Filling_station" title="Filling station">filling station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a></div></div></div>
<p>Biologically produced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohols" title="Alcohols">alcohols</a>, most commonly <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, and less commonly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propan-1-ol" title="Propan-1-ol">propanol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Butanol_fuel" title="Butanol fuel">butanol</a>, are produced by the action of <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> through the fermentation of sugars or starches (easiest), or cellulose (which is more difficult). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biobutanol" title="Biobutanol">Biobutanol</a> (also called biogasoline) is often claimed to provide a direct replacement for gasoline, because it can be used directly in a gasoline engine.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bushlulacana09032007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3506" data-file-width="2337" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bushlulacana09032007.jpg/220px-Bushlulacana09032007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bushlulacana09032007.jpg/330px-Bushlulacana09032007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bushlulacana09032007.jpg/440px-Bushlulacana09032007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bushlulacana09032007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> looks at sugar cane, a source of biofuel, with Brazilian President <a href="/wiki/Luiz_In%C3%A1cio_Lula_da_Silva" title="Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva">Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva</a> during a tour on biofuel technology at <a href="/wiki/Petrobras" title="Petrobras">Petrobras</a> in <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a>, Brazil, 9 March 2007.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">Ethanol fuel</a> is the most common biofuel worldwide, particularly <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="Ethanol fuel in Brazil">in Brazil</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_fuel" title="Alcohol fuel">Alcohol fuels</a> are produced by fermentation of sugars derived from <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">sugar beets</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">molasses</a> and any sugar or starch from which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcoholic beverages</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whiskey" title="Whiskey">whiskey</a>, can be made (such as <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a> waste, etc.). The ethanol production methods used are <a href="/wiki/Digestive_enzyme" title="Digestive enzyme">enzyme digestion</a> (to release sugars from stored starches), fermentation of the sugars, <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a> and drying. The distillation process requires significant energy input for heat (sometimes unsustainable <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> fossil fuel, but cellulosic biomass such as <a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">bagasse</a>, the waste left after sugar cane is pressed to extract its juice, is the most common fuel in Brazil, while pellets, wood chips and also <a href="/wiki/Waste_heat" title="Waste heat">waste heat</a> are more common in Europe) Waste steam fuels ethanol factory<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> – where waste heat from the factories also is used in the district heating grid.
</p><p>Ethanol can be used in petrol engines as a replacement for gasoline; it can be mixed with gasoline to any percentage. Most existing car petrol engines can run on blends of up to 15% bioethanol with petroleum/gasoline. Ethanol has a smaller <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> than that of gasoline; this means it takes more fuel (volume and mass) to produce the same amount of work. An advantage of ethanol (<span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>OH</span>) is that it has a higher <a href="/wiki/Octane_rating" title="Octane rating">octane rating</a> than ethanol-free gasoline available at roadside gas stations, which allows an increase of an engine's <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">compression ratio</a> for increased <a href="/wiki/Thermal_efficiency" title="Thermal efficiency">thermal efficiency</a>. In high-altitude (thin air) locations, some states mandate a mix of gasoline and ethanol as a winter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidizer" title="Oxidizer">oxidizer</a> to reduce atmospheric pollution emissions.
</p><p>Ethanol is also used to fuel bioethanol <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fireplaces" title="Fireplaces">fireplaces</a>. As they do not require a chimney and are "flueless", bioethanol fires<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> are extremely useful for newly built homes and apartments without a flue.
The downsides to these fireplaces is that their heat output is slightly less than electric heat or gas fires, and precautions must be taken to avoid carbon monoxide poisoning.
</p><p>Corn-to-ethanol and other food stocks has led to the development of <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">cellulosic ethanol</a>. According to a joint research agenda conducted through the US Department of Energy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> the fossil energy ratios (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fossil_Energy_Ratio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fossil Energy Ratio (page does not exist)">FER</a>) for cellulosic ethanol, corn ethanol, and gasoline are 10.3, 1.36, and 0.81, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ethanol has roughly one-third lower energy content per unit of volume compared to gasoline. This is partly counteracted by the better efficiency when using ethanol (in a long-term test of more than 2.1 million km, the BEST project found FFV vehicles to be 1–26% more energy efficient than petrol cars, but the volumetric consumption increases by approximately 30%, so more fuel stops are required).
</p><p>With current subsidies, ethanol fuel is slightly cheaper per distance traveled in the United States.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_bioalcohols">Other bioalcohols</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">Methanol</a> is currently produced from <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-renewable" title="Non-renewable">non-renewable</a> fossil fuel. In the future it is hoped to be produced from biomass as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomethanol" title="Biomethanol">biomethanol</a>. This is technically feasible, but the production is currently being postponed for concerns of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jacob_S._Gibbs&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jacob S. Gibbs (page does not exist)">Jacob S. Gibbs</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Brinsley_Coleberd&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Brinsley Coleberd (page does not exist)">Brinsley Coleberd</a> that the economic viability is still pending.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Methanol_economy" title="Methanol economy">methanol economy</a> is an alternative to the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">hydrogen economy</a>, compared to today's <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> production from natural gas.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Butanol_fuel" title="Butanol fuel">Butanol</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span>OH</span>) is formed by <a href="/wiki/Clostridium_acetobutylicum" title="Clostridium acetobutylicum">ABE fermentation</a> (acetone, butanol, ethanol) and experimental modifications of the process show potentially high <a href="/wiki/Net_energy_gain" title="Net energy gain">net energy gains</a> with butanol as the only liquid product. Butanol will produce more energy and allegedly can be burned "straight" in existing gasoline engines (without modification to the engine or car),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and is less corrosive and less water-soluble than ethanol, and could be distributed via existing infrastructures. <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a> and <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">BP</a> are working together to help develop butanol. <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i> strains have also been successfully engineered to produce butanol by modifying their <a href="/wiki/Protein_metabolism" title="Protein metabolism">amino acid metabolism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-butanol_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-butanol-36">[36]</a></sup> One drawback to butanol production in E. coli remains the high cost of nutrient rich media, however, recent work has demonstrated E. coli can produce butanol with minimal nutritional supplementation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biodiesel_around_the_world" title="Biodiesel around the world">Biodiesel around the world</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a> is the most common biofuel in Europe. It is produced from oils or fats using <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a> and is a liquid similar in composition to fossil/mineral diesel. Chemically, it consists mostly of fatty acid methyl (or ethyl) esters (<a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_methyl_ester" title="Fatty acid methyl ester">FAMEs</a>). Feedstocks for biodiesel include animal fats, vegetable oils, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soy" title="Soy">soy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rapeseed" title="Rapeseed">rapeseed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jatropha_curcas" title="Jatropha curcas">jatropha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madhuca_longifolia" title="Madhuca longifolia">mahua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mustard_plant" title="Mustard plant">mustard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunflower" title="Sunflower">sunflower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thlaspi_arvense" title="Thlaspi arvense">field pennycress</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pongamia_pinnata" title="Pongamia pinnata">Pongamia pinnata</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Algae_fuel" title="Algae fuel">algae</a>. Pure biodiesel (B100, also known as "neat" biodiesel) currently reduces emissions with up to 60% compared to diesel Second generation B100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Targray_Biodiesel_Railcar.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="470" data-file-width="1154" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Targray_Biodiesel_Railcar.png/220px-Targray_Biodiesel_Railcar.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Targray_Biodiesel_Railcar.png/330px-Targray_Biodiesel_Railcar.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Targray_Biodiesel_Railcar.png/440px-Targray_Biodiesel_Railcar.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Targray_Biodiesel_Railcar.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Targray Biofuels Division railcar transporting Biodiesel.</div></div></div><p>Biodiesel can be used in any <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engine</a> when mixed with mineral diesel. It can also be used in its pure form (B100) in diesel engines, but some maintenance and performance problems may then occur during wintertime utilization, since the fuel becomes somewhat more <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscous</a> at lower temperatures, depending on the feedstock used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In some countries, manufacturers cover their diesel engines under warranty for B100 use, although <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> of <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, for example, asks drivers to check by telephone with the VW environmental services department before switching to B100. In most cases, biodiesel is compatible with diesel engines from 1994 onwards, which use '<a href="/wiki/Viton" title="Viton">Viton</a>' (by <a href="/wiki/DuPont" title="DuPont">DuPont</a>) <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_rubber" title="Synthetic rubber">synthetic rubber</a> in their mechanical <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection" title="Fuel injection">fuel injection</a> systems. Note however, that no vehicles are certified for using pure biodiesel before 2014, as there was no emission control protocol available for biodiesel before this date.
</p><p>Electronically controlled '<a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a>' and '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unit_Injector" title="Unit Injector">unit injector</a>' type systems from the late 1990s onwards may only use biodiesel blended with conventional diesel fuel. These engines have finely metered and atomized multiple-stage injection systems that are very sensitive to the viscosity of the fuel. Many current-generation diesel engines are made so that they can run on B100 without altering the engine itself, although this depends on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_rail" title="Fuel rail">fuel rail</a> design.
Since biodiesel is an effective <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> and cleans residues deposited by mineral diesel, <a href="/wiki/Oil_filter" title="Oil filter">engine filters</a> may need to be replaced more often, as the biofuel dissolves old deposits in the fuel tank and pipes. It also effectively cleans the engine <a href="/wiki/Combustion_chamber" title="Combustion chamber">combustion chamber</a> of carbon deposits, helping to maintain efficiency. In many European countries, a 5% biodiesel blend is widely used and is available at thousands of gas stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Biodiesel is also an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygenated_diesel" title="Oxygenated diesel">oxygenated fuel</a>, meaning it contains a reduced amount of carbon and higher hydrogen and oxygen content than fossil diesel. This improves the <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> of biodiesel and reduces the particulate emissions from unburnt carbon. However, using pure biodiesel may increase NO<sub>x</sub>-emissions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biodiesel is also safe to handle and transport because it is non-toxic and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biodegradable" title="Biodegradable">biodegradable</a>, and has a high <a href="/wiki/Flash_point" title="Flash point">flash point</a> of about 300 °F (148 °C) compared to petroleum diesel fuel, which has a flash point of 125 °F (52 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the US, more than 80% of commercial trucks and city buses run on diesel. The emerging US biodiesel market is estimated to have grown 200% from 2004 to 2005. "By the end of 2006 biodiesel production was estimated to increase fourfold [from 2004] to more than" 1 billion US gallons (3,800,000 m<sup>3</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>In France, biodiesel is incorporated at a rate of 8% in the fuel used by all French diesel vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RA29_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-RA29-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Avril_Group" title="Avril Group">Avril Group</a> produces under the brand <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diester" title="Diester">Diester</a>, a fifth of 11 million tons of biodiesel consumed annually by the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EurObserv’ER_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-EurObserv’ER-46">[46]</a></sup> It is the leading European producer of biodiesel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RA29_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-RA29-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Green_diesel">Green diesel</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_refining" title="Vegetable oil refining">Vegetable oil refining</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_refining" title="Vegetable oil refining">Green diesel</a> is produced through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocracking" title="Hydrocracking">hydrocracking</a> biological oil feedstocks, such as vegetable oils and animal fats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-seven-48">[48]</a></sup> Hydrocracking is a refinery method that uses elevated temperatures and pressure in the presence of a catalyst to break down larger <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecules" title="Molecules">molecules</a>, such as those found in <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oils</a>, into shorter <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> chains used in <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alpha_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-alpha-49">[49]</a></sup> It may also be called renewable diesel, hydrotreated vegetable oil<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alpha_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-alpha-49">[49]</a></sup> or hydrogen-derived renewable diesel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-seven-48">[48]</a></sup> Unlike biodiesel, green diesel has exactly the same chemical properties as petroleum-based diesel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alpha_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-alpha-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> It does not require new engines, pipelines or infrastructure to distribute and use, but has not been produced at a cost that is competitive with <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-seven-48">[48]</a></sup> Gasoline versions are also being developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Green diesel is being developed in <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> by <a href="/wiki/ConocoPhillips" title="ConocoPhillips">ConocoPhillips</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neste_Oil" title="Neste Oil">Neste Oil</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valero_Energy_Corporation" title="Valero Energy Corporation">Valero</a>, Dynamic Fuels, and <a href="/wiki/UOP_LLC" title="UOP LLC">Honeywell UOP</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seven_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-seven-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> as well as Preem in Gothenburg, Sweden, creating what is known as Evolution Diesel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Straight_vegetable_oil">Straight vegetable oil</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:167px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Used_vegetable_cooking_oil.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="165" height="413" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a9/Used_vegetable_cooking_oil.png" width="165"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Used_vegetable_cooking_oil.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Filtered waste vegetable oil</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="2464" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg/220px-Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg/330px-Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg/440px-Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walmart%E2%80%99s_Grease_Fuel_Truck_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This truck is one of 15 based at Walmart's <a href="/wiki/Buckeye,_Arizona" title="Buckeye, Arizona">Buckeye, Arizona</a> distribution center that was converted to run on a biofuel made from reclaimed cooking grease produced during food preparation at Walmart stores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_fuel" title="Vegetable oil fuel">Vegetable oil fuel</a></div>
<p>Straight unmodified <a href="/wiki/Eating" title="Eating">edible</a> vegetable oil is generally not used as fuel, but lower-quality oil has been used for this purpose. Used vegetable oil is increasingly being processed into biodiesel, or (more rarely) cleaned of water and particulates and then used as a fuel.
</p><p>As with 100% biodiesel (B100), to ensure the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_injector" title="Fuel injector">fuel injectors</a> atomize the vegetable oil in the correct pattern for efficient combustion, <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_fuel" title="Vegetable oil fuel">vegetable oil fuel</a> must be heated to reduce its <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> to that of diesel, either by electric coils or heat exchangers. This is easier in warm or temperate climates. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MAN_B%26W_Diesel" title="MAN B&amp;W Diesel">MAN B&amp;W Diesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/W%C3%A4rtsil%C3%A4" title="Wärtsilä">Wärtsilä</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Deutz_AG" title="Deutz AG">Deutz AG</a>, as well as a number of smaller companies, such as <a href="/wiki/Elsbett" title="Elsbett">Elsbett</a>, offer engines that are compatible with straight vegetable oil, without the need for after-market modifications.
</p><p>Vegetable oil can also be used in many older diesel engines that do not use <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unit_Injector" title="Unit Injector">unit injection</a> electronic diesel injection systems. Due to the design of the combustion chambers in <a href="/wiki/Indirect_injection" title="Indirect injection">indirect injection</a> engines, these are the best engines for use with vegetable oil. This system allows the relatively larger oil molecules more time to burn. Some older engines, especially Mercedes, are driven experimentally by enthusiasts without any conversion, a handful of drivers have experienced limited success with earlier pre-"Pumpe Duse" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VW_TDI" title="VW TDI">VW TDI</a> engines and other similar engines with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_fuel_injection" title="Direct fuel injection">direct injection</a>. Several companies, such as <a href="/wiki/Elsbett" title="Elsbett">Elsbett</a> or Wolf, have developed professional conversion kits and successfully installed hundreds of them over the last decades.
</p><p>Oils and fats can be <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenated</a> to give a diesel substitute. The resulting product is a straight-chain hydrocarbon with a high <a href="/wiki/Cetane_number" title="Cetane number">cetane number</a>, low in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatics" title="Aromatics">aromatics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> and does not contain oxygen. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogenated_oil" title="Hydrogenated oil">Hydrogenated oils</a> can be blended with diesel in all proportions. They have several advantages over biodiesel, including good performance at low temperatures, no storage stability problems and no susceptibility to microbial attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evans_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-evans-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bioethers">Bioethers</span></h3>
<p>Bioethers (also referred to as fuel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethers" title="Ethers">ethers</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygenated_fuel" title="Oxygenated fuel">oxygenated fuels</a>) are cost-effective <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a> that act as <a href="/wiki/Octane_rating" title="Octane rating">octane rating</a> enhancers."Bioethers are produced by the reaction of reactive iso-olefins, such as iso-butylene, with bioethanol."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Bioethers are created by wheat or sugar beet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>  They also enhance <a href="/wiki/Engine" title="Engine">engine</a> performance, while significantly reducing engine wear and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">toxic</a> <a href="/wiki/Exhaust_gas" title="Exhaust gas">exhaust emissions</a>. Although bioethers are likely to replace petroethers in the UK, it is highly unlikely they will become a fuel in and of itself due to the low energy density.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>  Greatly reducing the amount of ground-level <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ozone_emissions&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ozone emissions (page does not exist)">ozone emissions</a>, they contribute to air quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>When it comes to transportation fuel there are six ether additives: <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_ether" title="Dimethyl ether">dimethyl ether</a> (DME), <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">diethyl ether</a> (DEE), methyl teritiary-butyl ether (MTBE), ethyl <i>ter</i>-butyl ether (ETBE), t<i>er</i>-amyl methyl ether (TAME), and <i>ter</i>-amyl ethyl ether (TAEE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The European Fuel Oxygenates Association (EFOA) credits methyl Ttertiary-butyl ether (MTBE) and ethyl ter-butyl ether (ETBE) as the most commonly used ethers in fuel to replace lead. Ethers were introduced in Europe in the 1970s to replace the highly toxic compound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Although Europeans still use bio-ether additives, the US no longer has an oxygenate requirement therefore bio-ethers are no longer used as the main fuel additive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solid_biomass_fuels">Solid biomass fuels</span></h3>
<p>Examples include <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sawdust" title="Sawdust">sawdust</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass" title="Grass">grass</a> trimmings, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_waste" title="Domestic waste">domestic refuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural_waste" title="Agricultural waste">agricultural waste</a>, nonfood <a href="/wiki/Energy_crop" title="Energy crop">energy crops</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dry_animal_dung_fuel" title="Dry animal dung fuel">dried manure</a>.
</p><p>When solid biomass is already in a suitable form (such as <a href="/wiki/Firewood" title="Firewood">firewood</a>), it can burn directly in a stove or furnace to provide heat or raise steam. When solid biomass is in an inconvenient form (such as sawdust, wood chips, grass, urban waste wood, agricultural residues), the typical process is to densify the biomass. This process includes grinding the raw biomass to an appropriate particulate size (known as hogfuel), which, depending on the densification type, can be from 1 to 3 cm (0.4 to 1.2 in), which is then concentrated into a fuel product. The current processes produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_pellet" title="Wood pellet">wood pellets</a>, cubes, or pucks. The pellet process is most common in Europe, and is typically a pure wood product. The other types of densification are larger in size compared to a pellet and are compatible with a broad range of input feedstocks. The resulting densified fuel is easier to transport and feed into thermal generation systems, such as boilers.
</p><p>Sawdust, bark and chips are already used for decades for fuel in industrial processes; examples include the pulp and paper industry and the sugar cane industry. Boilers in the range of 500,000 lb/hr of steam, and larger, are in routine operation, using grate, spreader stoker, suspension burning and fluid bed combustion. Utilities generate power, typically in the range of 5 to 50 MW, using locally available fuel. Other industries have also installed wood waste fueled boilers and dryers in areas with low-cost fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the advantages of solid biomass fuel is that it is often a byproduct, residue or waste-product of other processes, such as farming, animal husbandry and forestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-64">[64]</a></sup> In theory, this means fuel and food production do not compete for resources, although this is not always the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>A problem with the combustion of solid biomass fuels is that it emits considerable amounts of <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutants</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">particulates</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a>. Even modern pellet boilers generate much more pollutants than oil or natural gas boilers. Pellets made from agricultural residues are usually worse than wood pellets, producing much larger emissions of <a href="/wiki/Dioxins_and_dioxin-like_compounds" title="Dioxins and dioxin-like compounds">dioxins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorophenol" title="Chlorophenol">chlorophenols</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>A derived fuel is <a href="/wiki/Biochar" title="Biochar">biochar</a>, which is produced by biomass <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a>. Biochar made from agricultural waste can substitute for wood charcoal. As wood stock becomes scarce, this alternative is gaining ground. In eastern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a>, for example, <a href="/wiki/Biomass_briquettes" title="Biomass briquettes">biomass briquettes</a> are being marketed as an alternative to charcoal to protect <a href="/wiki/Virunga_National_Park" title="Virunga National Park">Virunga National Park</a> from <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> associated with <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_region">By region</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biofuels_by_region" title="Biofuels by region">Biofuels by region</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biodiesel_around_the_world" title="Biodiesel around the world">Biodiesel around the world</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3672" data-file-width="4896" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg/290px-Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg/435px-Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg/580px-Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bio_Diesel_Powered_Fast_Attack_Craft_Of_Indian_Navy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bio Diesel Powered Fast Attack Craft Of <a href="/wiki/Indian_Navy" title="Indian Navy">Indian Navy</a> patrolling during <a href="/wiki/International_Fleet_Review_2016" title="International Fleet Review 2016">IFR 2016</a>.The green bands on the vessels are indicative of the fact that the vessels are powered by <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">bio-diesel</a></div></div></div>
<p>There are international organizations such as IEA Bioenergy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> established in 1978 by the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> (IEA), with the aim of improving cooperation and information exchange between countries that have national programs in bioenergy research, development and deployment. The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a> International Biofuels Forum is formed by <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The world leaders in biofuel development and use are Brazil, the United States, France, Sweden and Germany. Russia also has 22% of world's forest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and is a big biomass (solid biofuels) supplier. In 2010, Russian pulp and paper maker, Vyborgskaya Cellulose, said they would be producing pellets that can be used in heat and electricity generation from its plant in Vyborg by the end of the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The plant will eventually produce about 900,000 tons of pellets per year, making it the largest in the world once operational.
</p><p>Biofuels currently make up 3.1%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> of the total road transport fuel in the UK or 1,440 million litres. By 2020, 10% of the energy used in UK road and rail transport must come from renewable sources – this is the equivalent of replacing 4.3 million tonnes of fossil oil each year. Conventional biofuels are likely to produce between 3.7 and 6.6% of the energy needed in road and rail transport, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_biofuels" title="Advanced biofuels">advanced biofuels</a> could meet up to 4.3% of the UK's renewable transport fuel target by 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nnfcc_adv_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-nnfcc_adv-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_pollution">Air pollution</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Biomass#Environmental_damage" title="Biomass">Biomass § Environmental damage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel#Air_pollution" title="Ethanol fuel">Ethanol_fuel § Air_pollution</a></div>
<p>Biofuels are similar to fossil fuels in that biofuels contribute to <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>. Burning produces <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, airborne carbon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particulate" title="Particulate">particulates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The WHO estimates 3.7 million premature deaths worldwide in 2012 due to air pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Brazil burns significant amounts of ethanol biofuel. Gas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromatograph" title="Chromatograph">chromatograph</a> studies were performed of ambient air in São Paulo, Brazil, and compared to Osaka, Japan, which does not burn ethanol fuel. Atmospheric Formaldehyde was 160% higher in Brazil, and Acetaldehyde was 260% higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Environmental Protection Agency has acknowledged in April 2007 that the increased use of bio-ethanol will lead to worse air quality. The total emissions of air pollutants such as nitrogen oxides will rise due the growing use of bio-ethanol. There is an increase in carbon dioxide from the burning of fossil fuels to produce the biofuels as well as nitrous oxide from the soil, which has most likely been treated with nitrogen fertilizer. Nitrous oxide is known to have a greater impact on the atmosphere in relation to global warming, as it is also an ozone destroyer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Debates_regarding_the_production_and_use_of_biofuel">Debates regarding the production and use of biofuel</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Issues_relating_to_biofuels" title="Issues relating to biofuels">Issues relating to biofuels</a></div>
<p>There are various social, economic, environmental and technical issues with biofuel production and use, which have been discussed in the popular media and scientific journals. These include: the effect of moderating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Price_of_petroleum" title="Price of petroleum">oil prices</a>, the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_vs_fuel" title="Food vs fuel">food vs fuel</a>" debate, <a href="/wiki/Food_prices" title="Food prices">food prices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poverty_reduction" title="Poverty reduction">poverty reduction</a> potential, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_ratio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Energy ratio (page does not exist)">energy ratio</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_requirements&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Energy requirements (page does not exist)">energy requirements</a>,  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a> levels, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_biofuel" title="Sustainable biofuel">sustainable biofuel</a> production, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a>, loss of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> impact on <a href="/wiki/Water_resources" title="Water resources">water resources</a>, the possible modifications necessary to run the engine on biofuel, as well as <a href="/wiki/Energy_returned_on_energy_invested" title="Energy returned on energy invested">energy balance</a> and efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">International Resource Panel</a>, which provides independent scientific assessments and expert advice on a variety of resource-related themes, assessed the issues relating to biofuel use in its first report <i>Towards sustainable production and use of resources: Assessing Biofuels</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> "Assessing Biofuels" outlined the wider and interrelated factors that need to be considered when deciding on the relative merits of pursuing one biofuel over another. It concluded that not all biofuels perform equally in terms of their impact on climate, energy security and ecosystems, and suggested that environmental and social impacts need to be assessed throughout the entire life-cycle.
</p><p>Another issue with biofuel use and production is the US has changed mandates many times because the production has been taking longer than expected. The Renewable Fuel Standard (RFS) set by congress for 2010 was pushed back to at best 2012 to produce 100 million gallons of pure ethanol (not blended with a fossil fuel).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banning_of_first-generation_biofuels">Banning of first-generation biofuels</span></h3>
<p>In the EU, the revised renewable energy directive calls for a complete ban on first-generation biofuels by 2030. Particularly fuels made from such oils such as palm oil and soy oil are being targeted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainable_biofuels">Sustainable biofuels</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sustainable_biofuels" title="Sustainable biofuels">Sustainable biofuels</a></div>
<p>Many of the biofuels that were being supplied in 2008 (using the first-generation biofuel production procedure) have been criticised for their adverse impacts on the <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">natural environment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land use</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roy_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-roy-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sol_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-sol-85">[85]</a></sup> In 2008, the Nobel-prize winning chemist <a href="/wiki/Paul_J._Crutzen" title="Paul J. Crutzen">Paul J. Crutzen</a> published findings that the release of <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (N<sub>2</sub>O) emissions in the production of biofuels means that overall they contribute more to global warming than the fossil fuels they replace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
In 2008, the challenge was to support biofuel development, including the development of new <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">cellulosic technologies</a>, with responsible policies and economic instruments to help ensure that biofuel commercialization is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sustainable" title="Sustainable">sustainable</a>. Responsible commercialization of biofuels represented an opportunity to enhance sustainable economic prospects in Africa, Latin America and Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roy_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-roy-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sol_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-sol-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
Now, biofuels in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_fuels" title="Liquid fuels">liquid fuels</a> derived from plant materials are entering the market, driven by the perception that they reduce climate gas emissions, and also by factors such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_price_increases_since_2003" title="Oil price increases since 2003">oil price spikes</a> and the need for increased <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">energy security</a>.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountain_Institute" title="Rocky Mountain Institute">Rocky Mountain Institute</a>, sound biofuel production practices would not hamper food and fibre production, nor cause water or environmental problems, and would enhance soil fertility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The selection of land on which to grow the feedstocks is a critical component of the ability of biofuels to deliver sustainable solutions. A key consideration is the minimisation of biofuel competition for prime cropland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-royal_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-royal-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gas_emissions">Greenhouse gas emissions</span></h3>
<p>Some scientists have expressed concerns about land-use change in response to greater demand for crops to use for biofuel and the subsequent carbon emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science-91">[91]</a></sup> The payback period, that is, the time it will take biofuels to pay back the carbon debt they acquire due to land-use change, has been estimated to be between 100 and 1000 years, depending on the specific instance and location of land-use change. However, no-till practices combined with cover-crop practices can reduce the payback period to three years for grassland conversion and 14 years for forest conversion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ES&amp;T_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-ES&amp;T-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>A study conducted in the Tocantis State, in northern Brazil, found that many families were cutting down forests in order to produce two conglomerates of oilseed plants, the J. curcas (JC group) and the R. communis (RC group). This region is composed of 15% Amazonian rainforest with high biodiversity, and 80% Cerrado forest with lower biodiversity. During the study, the farmers that planted the JC group released over 2193 Mg CO<sub>2</sub>, while losing 53-105 Mg CO<sub>2</sub> sequestration from deforestation; and the RC group farmers released 562 Mg CO<sub>2</sub>, while losing 48-90 Mg CO<sub>2</sub> to be sequestered from forest depletion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The production of these types of biofuels not only led into an increased emission of carbon dioxide, but also to lower efficiency of forests to absorb the gases that these farms were emitting. This has to do with the amount of fossil fuel the production of fuel crops involves. In addition, the intensive use of monocropping agriculture requires large amounts of water irrigation, as well as of fertilizers, herbicides and pesticides. This does not only lead to poisonous chemicals to disperse on water runoff, but also to the emission of nitrous oxide (NO<sub>2</sub>) as a fertilizer byproduct, which is three hundred times more efficient in producing a greenhouse effect than carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Converting rainforests, peatlands, savannas, or grasslands to produce food crop–based biofuels in Brazil, Southeast Asia, and the United States creates a “biofuel carbon debt” by releasing 17 to 420 times more CO<sub>2</sub> than the annual greenhouse gas (GHG) reductions that these biofuels would provide by displacing fossil fuels. Biofuels made from waste biomass or from biomass grown on abandoned agricultural lands incur little to no carbon debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science2_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science2-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_Use">Water Use</span></h3>
<p>In addition to crop growth requiring water, biofuel facilities require significant process water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaterI_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-WaterI-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_research">Current research</span></h2>
<p>Research is ongoing into finding more suitable biofuel crops and improving the oil yields of these crops. Using the current yields, vast amounts of land and fresh water would be needed to produce enough oil to completely replace fossil fuel usage. It would require twice the land area of the US to be devoted to soybean production, or two-thirds to be devoted to rapeseed production, to meet current US heating and transportation needs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Specially bred mustard varieties can produce reasonably high oil yields and are very useful in <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a> with cereals, and have the added benefit that the meal left over after the oil has been pressed out can act as an effective and biodegradable pesticide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NFESC" title="NFESC">NFESC</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Santa_Barbara,_California" title="Santa Barbara, California">Santa Barbara</a>-based Biodiesel Industries, is working to develop biofuels technologies for the US navy and military, one of the largest diesel fuel users in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
A group of Spanish developers working for a company called <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ecofasa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ecofasa (page does not exist)">Ecofasa</a> announced a new biofuel made from trash. The fuel is created from general urban waste which is treated by bacteria to produce fatty acids, which can be used to make biofuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Before its shutdown, <a href="/wiki/Joule_Unlimited" title="Joule Unlimited">Joule Unlimited</a> was attempting to make cheap ethanol and biodiesel from a genetically modified photosynthetic bacterium.
</p>
<h3><span id="Ethanol_biofuels_.28bioethanol.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethanol_biofuels_(bioethanol)">Ethanol biofuels (bioethanol)<span id="Ethanol_biofuels"></span><span id="Bioethanol"></span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">Ethanol fuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol_commercialization" title="Cellulosic ethanol commercialization">Cellulosic ethanol commercialization</a></div>
<p>As the primary source of biofuels in North America, many organizations are conducting research in the area of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> production. The National Corn-to-Ethanol Research Center (NCERC) is a research division of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Illinois_University_Edwardsville" title="Southern Illinois University Edwardsville">Southern Illinois University Edwardsville</a> dedicated solely to ethanol-based biofuel research projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
On the federal level, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USDA" title="USDA">USDA</a> conducts a large amount of research regarding ethanol production in the United States. Much of this research is targeted toward the effect of ethanol production on domestic food markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> A division of the U.S. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">Department of Energy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> (NREL), has also conducted various ethanol research projects, mainly in the area of cellulosic ethanol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol_commercialization" title="Cellulosic ethanol commercialization">Cellulosic ethanol commercialization</a> is the process of building an industry out of methods of turning cellulose-containing organic matter into fuel. Companies, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iogen_Corp." title="Iogen Corp.">Iogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/POET" title="POET">POET</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abengoa" title="Abengoa">Abengoa</a>, are building refineries that can process <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> and turn it into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioethanol" title="Bioethanol">bioethanol</a>. Companies, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diversa" title="Diversa">Diversa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novozymes" title="Novozymes">Novozymes</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dyadic_International&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dyadic International (page does not exist)">Dyadic</a>, are producing enzymes that could enable a <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">cellulosic ethanol</a> future. The shift from food crop feedstocks to waste residues and native grasses offers significant opportunities for a range of players, from farmers to biotechnology firms, and from project developers to investors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2013, the first commercial-scale plants to produce cellulosic biofuels have begun operating. Multiple pathways for the conversion of different biofuel feedstocks are being used. In the next few years, the cost data of these technologies operating at commercial scale, and their relative performance, will become available. Lessons learnt will lower the costs of the industrial processes involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>In parts of Asia and Africa where drylands prevail, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_sorghum" title="Sweet sorghum">sweet sorghum</a> is being investigated as a potential source of food, feed and fuel combined. The crop is particularly suitable for growing in arid conditions, as it only extracts one seventh of the water used by sugarcane. In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and other places, sweet sorghum stalks are used to produce biofuel by squeezing the juice and then fermenting into <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>A study by researchers at the <a href="/wiki/International_Crops_Research_Institute_for_the_Semi-Arid_Tropics" title="International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics">International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICRISAT" title="ICRISAT">ICRISAT</a>) found that growing sweet sorghum instead of grain sorghum could increase farmers incomes by US$40 per hectare per crop because it can provide fuel in addition to food and animal feed. With grain sorghum currently grown on over 11 million hectares (ha) in Asia and on 23.4 million ha in Africa, a switch to sweet sorghum could have a considerable economic impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jatropha">Jatropha</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jatropha_biofuel" title="Jatropha biofuel">Jatropha biofuel</a></div>
<p>Several groups in various sectors are conducting research on <i><a href="/wiki/Jatropha_curcas" title="Jatropha curcas">Jatropha curcas</a></i>, a poisonous shrub-like tree that produces seeds considered by many to be a viable source of biofuels feedstock oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Much of this research focuses on improving the overall per acre oil yield of Jatropha through advancements in genetics, soil science, and horticultural practices.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/SG_Biofuels" title="SG Biofuels">SG Biofuels</a>, a San Diego-based jatropha developer, has used molecular breeding and biotechnology to produce elite hybrid seeds that show significant yield improvements over first-generation varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> SG Biofuels also claims additional benefits have arisen from such strains, including improved flowering synchronicity, higher resistance to pests and diseases, and increased cold-weather tolerance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plant Research International, a department of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wageningen_University_and_Research_Centre" title="Wageningen University and Research Centre">Wageningen University and Research Centre</a> in the Netherlands, maintains an ongoing Jatropha Evaluation Project that examines the feasibility of large-scale jatropha cultivation through field and laboratory experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
The Center for Sustainable Energy Farming (CfSEF) is a Los Angeles-based nonprofit research organization dedicated to jatropha research in the areas of plant science, agronomy, and horticulture. Successful exploration of these disciplines is projected to increase jatropha farm production yields by 200-300% in the next 10 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fungi">Fungi</span></h3>
<p>A group at the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Russian Academy of Sciences">Russian Academy of Sciences</a> in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, in a 2008 paper, stated they had isolated large amounts of lipids from single-celled fungi and turned it into biofuels in an economically efficient manner. More research on this fungal species, <i><a href="/wiki/Cunninghamella" title="Cunninghamella">Cunninghamella</a></i> <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cunninghamella_japonica&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cunninghamella japonica (page does not exist)">japonica</a></i>, and others, is likely to appear in the near future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
The recent discovery of a variant of the fungus <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gliocladium_roseum" title="Gliocladium roseum">Gliocladium roseum</a></i> (later renamed Ascocoryne sarcoides) points toward the production of so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myco-diesel" title="Myco-diesel">myco-diesel</a> from cellulose. This organism was recently discovered in the rainforests of northern <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a>, and has the unique capability of converting cellulose into medium-length hydrocarbons typically found in diesel fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Many other fungi that can degrade cellulose and other polymers have been observed to produce molecules that are currently being engineered using organisms from other kingdoms, suggesting that fungi may play a large role in the bio-production of fuels in the future (reviewed in <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_gut_bacteria">Animal gut bacteria</span></h3>
<p>Microbial <a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">gastrointestinal flora</a> in a variety of animals have shown potential for the production of biofuels. Recent research has shown that TU-103, a strain of <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium" title="Clostridium">Clostridium</a></i> bacteria found in Zebra feces, can convert nearly any form of cellulose into butanol fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Microbes in panda waste are being investigated for their use in creating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels" title="Biofuels">biofuels</a> from bamboo and other plant materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> There has also been substantial research into the technology of using the gut microbiomes of wood-feeding insects for the conversion of lignocellulotic material into biofuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An example here is <a href="/wiki/Rapeseed" title="Rapeseed">rapeseed</a>, of which the oil is used as waste fuel, and of which the actual produce (for which the crop is grown) is <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">fodder</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Coconut_oil" title="Coconut oil">Coconut oil</a> is another example, if it is extracted in such a way that the meat and milk can still be used for human and/or animal consumption.</span>
</li>

</li>
